Air Force, Air Force Materiel Command, Air Force Life Cycle Management Center, Business and Enterprise Systems Directorate requirement for operations support for the Acquisition Due-In System (ADIS) and Contracting Information System Database (CIDS) programs.</p>\n\n<h4>BACKGROUND</h4>\n\n<p>The Program Office for ADIS and CIDS is located at WPAFB, OH and is responsible for the daily operations and maintenance of the logistics business systems associated with the Air Force (AF) acquisition process. The scope of services for this contract is to provide program operations support for the ADIS and CIDS programs.</p>\n\n<p>The Acquisition Due-In System (ADIS) (J041) program provides a single repository of information for items centrally procured at the Air Logistics Complexes (ALCs). ADIS maintains and processes data for contracting and requirements activities from Purchase Request (PR) initiation (pre-Award) through the contract lifecycle to contract closeout (post-Award). Additionally, ADIS maintains historical data on stock numbered items. ADIS uses Continuous Capability Delivery (CCD) to execute sustainment. The Air Force Life Cycle Management Center (AFLCMC) Business and Enterprise Systems (BES) Directorate, Enterprise Logistics Readiness Portfolio (HI4) Supply (HIS), Logistics, Purchasing and Contracting Product Line (LPC) maintains ADIS in a fully-operational state and does as-needed patch releases to correct any deficiencies discovered in the current code base and to keep the application compliant with current statutory and regulatory guidelines. ADIS is a financial feeder system making it subject to an annual Financial Improvement and Audit Readiness (FIAR) review by an Independent Private Auditor (IPA). The application is hosted at Defense Information Systems Agency (DISA) Defense Enterprise Core Computing (DECC) Ogden, UT on an International Business Machine Multiple Virtual Storage (IBM MVS) mainframe platform.</p>\n\n<p>The Contracting Information Database System (CIDS)(J018R) program provides the ALCs with an on-line Ad Hoc query solution utilizing a desktop client application and an Oracle relational database management system server. CIDS provides clients with the data necessary to improve the Air Force Materiel Command (AFMC) acquisition process and expedite PR processing. CIDS provides capabilities for the Item Managers to view the National Item Identification Number (NIIN) Item History record and provides Buyers and Item Managers a view into the central contracting processes by providing an automated method of reviewing contract data derived from ADIS and contract related data from Mechanization of Contract Administration Services (MOCAS) (Closeout), Wide Area Work Flow (WAWF) (Shipment/Acceptance) and D035A (Receipts). CIDS uses CCD to execute sustainment. CIDS uses the Agile software development approach to develop and maintain interfaces with interface partners and to keep the application compliant with current statutory and regulatory guidelines. CIDS is a financial feeder system making it subject to an annual FIAR review by an IPA. The client application is installed on desktops at the ALCs and connects via SQLNet to an Oracle database hosted on a MS Windows server located at Wright-Patterson Air Force Base (WPAFB), OH.</p>\n\n<h4>REQUIREMENTS</h4>\n\n<p><strong>ADIS (J041) Capability Support</strong></p>\n\n<ul>\n\t<li>\n\t<p>The Contractor shall provide personnel with knowledge and capabilities in Common Assessment Module (SAM)/D087C, a classified system maintained and operated at Defense Information Systems Agency (DISA), provides unit, wing and theater level war plan weapon system logistics assessments. SAM forecasts wartime sortie and mission generation based on existing logistics resource levels. SAM also forecasts the expected number of available aircraft on any given day of war and identifies specific factors limiting war-fighting capabilities. SAM accomplishes limited &ldquo;What-If&rdquo; assessment capability in support of and in response to operations planner decision processes</li>\r\n\t\t\t<li>\r\n\t\t\t\tRequirements/Execution Availability Logistics Module (REALM)/D087H, an unclassified system maintained and operated at GCSS-AF, is the Air Force corporate database for Readiness Spares Packages (RSP) and High Priority Mission Support Kits (HPMSK). REALM facilitates the exchange of RSP and HPMSK information among Air Force commands and organizations. REALM supports reviews of the range of items in RSP and HPMSK and computes the depth of spares requirements for RSP. REALM provides approved requirements for combat capability assessment, requisitioning, budgeting, and projecting requirements</li>\r\n\t\t\t<li>\r\n\t\t\t\tPropulsion Requirements System (PRS)/D087Q, a classified stand alone system maintained and operated at Wright-Patterson Air Force Base, Dayton, OH, has a central database of programming data and automates the process of determining unit-specific base stock levels for acquisition and distribution of engines. Specifically, PRS computes spare engine levels and overhaul requirements and provides Engine Acquisition Managers (EAM) with the capability to compute buying requirements for new engines</li>\r\n\t\t\t<li>\r\n\t\t\t\tRSP/Readiness Computation and Assessment System (RCAS)/D087R, an unclassified system maintained and operated at DISA, provides assessment support for the combat readiness of a weapon system to launch the first combat sortie, including aircraft engines, and recoverable spares required to conduct wartime missions. RCAS accomplishes assessments by comparing actual inventory, status, and utilization data with availability and mission capability requirements. Readiness can be analyzed for each weapon system at the mission/design/series, command, and base/unit level. RCAS focuses on historical, current, peacetime, and projected short-term asset availability. RCAS identifies the readiness and sustainment limiting items so they can be passed to the Supportability Analysis Visibility (SAV)/D087V module</li>\r\n\t\t\t<li>\r\n\t\t\t\tSAV/D087V, an unclassified system maintained and operated at DISA, provides Mission Capable (MICAP), Awaiting Parts (AWP), Air Force Readiness Driver Program (AFRDP), Maintenance and Weapon System analysis and reports. This modern, web-based, single-thread platform system integrates this cross section of logistics data to provide the warfighter a single decision support application and analysis capability. As the Air Force tool for the AFRDP, Weapon System and Inventory Managers actively identify, manage and report on Chief, Joint Chiefs of Staff (CJCS) project coded/selected High Impact Target (HIT) items for Readiness Drivers to improve overall warfighters&rsquo; supportability of critical parts shortages</li>\r\n\t\t\t<li>\r\n\t\t\t\tExecution and Prioritization of Repairs Support System (EXPRESS)/D087X is an automated tool that supports the Depot Repair Enhancement Program (DREP) and Agile Logistics efforts. EXPRESS provides a single integrated, centralized priority list of all Air Force repair requirements at all Air Logistics Centers (ALCs); determines the ability of existing resources to support repair actions; and provides the data and the mechanism to move items into repair. EXPRESS is maintained and operated at the DISA facility located at Dayton, OH, and base Network Operations Support Centers at Oklahoma City Air Logistics Center, Tinker Air Force Base, OK (OC-ALC), Ogden Air Logistics Center, Hill Air Force Base, UT (OO-ALC), and Warner Robins Air Logistics Center, Robins Air Force Base, GA (WR-ALC)</li>\r\n\t\t\t<li>\r\n\t\t\t\tTRACKER/D087T, an organically operated and maintained system, provides comprehensive supply, transportation, acquisition, and commercial movement information with the primary focus being pipeline performance analysis and transportation billing validation. TRACKER&rsquo;s database allows individual requisitions/shipments to be tracked via the World Wide Web. TRACKER functionality is maintained and operated by 754 ELSG/IL and 643 ELSS personnel at Wright-Patterson Air Force Base, Dayton, OH. However, no support will be required for this WSMIS system</li>\r\n\t\t\t<li>\r\n\t\t\t\tWSMIS-DB/D087W, an unclassified database maintained and operated at DISA, is an Oracle relational database that supports RCAS/D087R and SAV/D087V. fulfilling the Department of the Air Force, Air Force Materiel Command, Air Force Research Laboratory, 711th Human Performance Wing, USAF School of Aerospace Medicine (USAFSAM) continuing requirement for&nbsp;Aeromedical Aviation Laboratory (AAL) program.</p>\n\n<h4>BACKGROUND</h4>\n\n<p>The United States Air Force School of Aerospace Medicine (USAFSAM) located at Wright-Patterson AFB (WPAFB), Ohio, trains 100% of the USAF&rsquo;s Flight Surgeons in an Air Force Specialty Code (AFSC) awarding course entitled Aerospace Medicine Primary (AMP). The AMP course is composed of three increments: AMP 101, AMP 201, and AMP 202. AMP increments 101 and 202 utilize an Aeromedical Aviation Laboratory (AAL) to introduce AMP students to military flight operations. The Aeromedical Aviation Laboratory curriculum is specifically designed to expose flight surgeon students to the rigors of flying, mission planning, modern cockpit symbology, disorientation, instrument procedures, aerobatics, low-level navigation, and formation flight in a setting that closely mirrors USAF Undergraduate Flight Training (UFT). AAL provides each AMP 101 student with one flight sortie of one hour duration and AMP 202 students with 6 sorties totaling 8 flight hours. The purpose of the AAL program is to ensure all flight surgeon candidates receive flight experiences and complete the syllabus at Appendix A-2.</p>\n\n<p>The USAFSAM also sponsors a resident in aerospace medicine (RAM) program designed to enhance, motivate, and prepare these RAMs to make sound aeromedical decisions regarding aircrew member&rsquo;s fitness to fly, to evaluate physiological threats in the flying environment, and to research, identify, and design performance enhancements for aerospace vehicles and personnel. The AAL Aviation Competency Program (ACP) was developed to help accomplish these objectives. The ACP is a two year program and is broken out into two modules A and B. Module A includes 27 hours of academic training and provides each RAM with 14 sorties totaling 19.5 flight hours. Module B includes 22 hours of academic training and provides each second year RAM an additional 5 sorties totaling 7.5 flight hours of more advanced training.</p>\n\n<h4>REQUIREMENTS</h4>\n\n<p>The Contractor shall provide all services necessary to conduct the USAFSAM Aeromedical Aviation Laboratory (AAL). Quantity"}],"country":"USA","createdDate":"2020-04-06T14:17:36.300","description":"<p>InfoScitex Corp. and&nbsp;Stellar Science Ltd. are&nbsp;fulfilling the Department of the Air Force, Air Force Materiel Command, Air Force Research Laboratory, Aerospace Systems Directorate,&nbsp;Aerospace Vehicle Technology Assessment and Simulation (AVTAS) branch&nbsp;requirement for&nbsp;Redefining the Basic Analytical and Simulation Environment (REBASE) program research.</p>\n\n<h4>BACKGROUND</h4>\n\n<p>The Aerospace Vehicle Technology Assessment and Simulation (AVTAS) branch in the Air Force Research Laboratory (AFRL) currently develops, maintains and distributes the Advanced Framework for Simulation, Integration, and Modeling (AFSIM), a modular, object-oriented Multi-Domain Operations (MDO) Modeling, Simulation, &amp; Analysis (MS&amp;A) framework. AFRL intends for AFSIM to support multi-domain, multi-resolution MS&amp;A activities ranging from engineering to mission-level into campaign. Beyond the framework itself, AFRL develops and maintains an extensive AFSIM-compatible toolset including applications to ease scenario and model creation, improve results visualization, and enhance operator-in/on-the-loop interactions.</p>\n\n<p>AFSIM and its ecosystem of tools have evolved significantly over the past 15 years. Created out of frustration with legacy Air Force MS&amp;A tools, AFSIM originally provided a flexible solution to study advanced technology concepts within the air domain at the mission-level. AFRL has since expanded AFSIM&rsquo;s capabilities to partially address all warfighting domains (air, land, sea, space, cyber) as well as spectrum of simulation needs from the engineering-level to the campaign-level. The AFSIM community, including government and industry users across the DoD and Intelligence Community (IC), have encouraged these investments by continuing to supply AFRL with new and challenging requirements. Meanwhile, growing interest in AFSIM has postured the framework to become a common tool in the DoD&rsquo;s MS&amp;A toolbox. To date, AFRL has distributed AFSIM to over 300 government and industry organizations and trained over 1500 AFSIM users and developers.</p>\n\n<h4>REQUIREMENTS</h4>\n\n<ul>\n\t<li>Support comprehensive MDO MS&amp;A for the rapid study of emerging military technology concepts. Rich multi-domain representations help technologists, engineers, and analysts better assess military utility, evaluate architectural tradeoffs, and make informed engineering decisions</li>\n\t<li>Enhance Air Combat Operations Capabilities\n\t<ul>\n\t\t<li>Beginning with its legacy heritage and continued through Air Force sponsorship, AFSIM has incrementally improved its representation of air assets and their agency through combat behaviors and tactics. Investments to-date have helped reduce the time required to develop new aircraft and missile models sensitive to physics-based effects (e.g., aerodynamics, aircraft handling characteristics, propulsion), imbue those models with script-based perception and cognition, and understand their performance through visualization and analysis tools. Continued expansion and maturation of these investments will ensure that AFSIM can meaningfully contribute to the development next-generation air dominance capabilities. This initiative focuses on building up AFSIM&rsquo;s catalog of air asset models, expanding its library of modular air combat behaviors and tactics, and improving the methods and tools by which end-users can generate, compose, and explore those behaviors.</li>\n\t</ul>\n\t</li>\n\t<li>Enhance Space Operations Capabilities\n\t<ul>\n\t\t<li>Spaceborne capabilities have become an integral part of modern terrestrial warfare.&nbsp; As a result, mission and campaign-level simulation requires increasingly more detail in the depiction of space operations. The ubiquity of space effects on all warfighting domains drives a real need for &ldquo;boxset&rdquo; space capabilities to accommodate multiple levels of fidelity and degrees of configurability. While some end-users need authoritative, &ldquo;plug-n-play&rdquo; model assets to build a plausible space context, others require more flexibility to explore system-of-system tradespaces characterized by the performance of these spaceborne assets. Over the past several years, AFRL has invested significantly in improving AFSIM&rsquo;s representation of the space domain. Capabilities range from space weather modeling, to common propagator models, to visual tools that aid in the rapid creation of satellite constellations. This initiative intends to expand AFSIM&rsquo;s space operations capabilities in the form of models and supporting tools while balancing needs across the user community.</li>\n\t</ul>\n\t</li>\n\t<li>Enhance Cyber and EW Operations Capabilities\n\t<ul>\n\t\t<li>Success in modern warfare depends upon the control and flow of information, the ability to disrupt or influence an adversary&rsquo;s decision-making process, and the capacity to defend against the same. In order to maintain strategic advantage, the DoD continues to emphasize the criticality of information operations characterized by dominance of the entire electromagnetic spectrum and vital communications, command, and control networks and systems (JP 3-51). The capacity of U.S. adversaries to sense or affect the electromagnetic spectrum and vital defense systems presents a particular challenge for scientists, engineers, and analysts developing future weapon systems. Likewise, new materiel solutions (including both red and blue systems) often present new attack surfaces, exploited exclusively through the electromagnetic/information environment. AFSIM has traditionally represented the aggregate mission-level effects of electromagnetic and information operations on modeled systems and networks. While this level of fidelity has satisfied basic operational effectiveness studies, it strains to support studies that require more spatially and temporally accurate detail about the electromagnetic/information environment and interactions therein. This initiative intends to improve the capacity of &ldquo;boxset&rdquo; models to participate in these contested environments while providing end-users with a powerful set of analytical tools to master its effects.</li>\n\t</ul>\n\t</li>\n\t<li>Expand Threat Representations\n\t<ul>\n\t\t<li>The DoD has steadily increased its reliance on MS&amp;A to make significant S&amp;T investment and acquisition decisions. With limited resources and significant opportunity cost at stake, decision makers have likewise raised the bar for technologists and analysts to defend the credibility of their analyses. A successful defense generally begins with the use of traceable, pedigreed, validated, or otherwise authoritative, intelligence-derived models and scenarios. The AFSIM &ldquo;boxset&rdquo; should provide both end-users and decision makers alike with the evidence necessary to substantiate critical claims and arguments expressed through simulation. Ideally, system owners and intelligence agencies would provide end-users with comprehensive data on both foreign and friendly system-of-systems representations. Unfortunately, the DoD has yet to establish an accessible model clearinghouse inclusive of all warfighting domains and concerns. Where data exists, end-users must often make informed inferences or &ldquo;port&rdquo; existing models between frameworks and simulation environments. These translation steps introduce new sources of error and analytical doubt. To improve analytical credibility, AFRL intends to focus on the continuous and incremental expansion of authoritative, intelligence-derived threat representations and scenarios in AFSIM. These threat representations should come from supported IC model product lines, ensuring that the IC remain the recognized source for authoritative, intelligence-derived models in AFSIM. Model production should include commensurate investment in the tooling and processes necessary to accelerate threat modeling, integration, and verification in AFSIM.</li>\n\t</ul>\n\t</li>\n\t<li>Expand Joint Operations Capabilities\n\t<ul>\n\t\t<li>AFRL intends for AFSIM to become a commodity tool useful across the entire defense M&amp;SA community. Toward this end, the AFSIM user community has grown significantly over the past seven years and now includes representation across all military services and intelligence production centers. While AFRL does not intend to fashion AFSIM as a replacement for service and domain-specific models, it does intend to improve its representation of joint operations capabilities. More specifically, a meaningful pivot towards all-domain analysis requires balanced attention to the maritime and land operational domains. Historically underrepresented in AFSIM &ldquo;boxset&rdquo; scenarios and models, investments in these domains can help &ldquo;unlock&rdquo; new capabilities while re-imagining the future of U.S. combat operations.</li>\n\t</ul>\n\t</li>\n</ul>\n","duration":"4 years","govEntity":{"id":129663,"title":"AEROSPACE SYSTEMS DIRECTORATE"},"id":"OPP191140","iqOppId":191140,"oppValue":48000,"primaryNAICS":{"id":"541715","title":"Research and Development in the Physical, Engineering, and Life Sciences (except Nanotechnology and Biotechnology)","sizeStandard":"1000 Employees except 1500 Employees for Aircraft, Aircraft Engine and Engine Parts, 1250 Employees for Other Aircraft Parts and Auxiliary Equipment and 1300 Employees for Guided Missiles and Space Vehicles, Their Propulsion Units and Propu"},"primaryRequirement":"Research & Development","procurement":"<p>According to a Defense article released on June 29, 2021, Stellar Science Ltd. LLC is fulfilling the Department of the Air Force, Air Force Materiel Command, Air Force Life Cycle Management Center, Business and Enterprise Systems Directorate&nbsp;continuing requirement for Depot Wright-Patterson AFB OH 45433-5635. The primary purpose of the DM CODB is to consolidate and provide data for the production management of the Depot repair and maintenance process for the Air Force Enterprise. It is an operational-level data environment, serving on-line transaction processing applications for managing day-to-day operations in the Air Force aircraft maintenance depots. As these applications were modernized, technically refreshed, or rehosted, their data was loaded into the DM CODB. This environment then became their common source of data, receives their data updates, and sends and receives their data interfaces. The DM CODB does not absorb any of the primary functional logic of the DMSI application systems, but performs their data retrieval and updates, their interfaces, and scripts needed to produce the interfaces. Through these operational applications, the DM CODB supports AFMC users worldwide, including inventory management specialists, maintenance specialists, equipment specialists, fund monitors, and quality assurance specialists.<br />\n<br />\nThe DM CODB provides corporate-level data to support AFMC tactical and strategic planning activities. As operational applications are technically refreshed, they are enhanced with new user capabilities for reporting and on-line analytical processing (OLAP). Drawing upon the DM CODB&rsquo;s consolidated data, these systems enable users to extract information, perform analyses, create reports, and respond to information requests from AFMC command-level staff, HQ Air Force staff, and Department of Defense (DoD) elements. The DM CODB also provides data for similar purposes to the Air Force Global Combat Support System Data Services (GCSS-AF DS), and to other data marts / warehouses and Enterprise Integration projects. The DM CODB is based upon and compliant with the Joint Technical Architecture (JTA), and the Air Force Global Combat Support System (GCSS-AF) architecture.</p>\n\n<h4>REQUIREMENTS&nbsp;</h4>\n\n<ul>\n\t<li>The requirement is to Provide database modification and support to DMCODB, including surveillance of batch jobs, maintaining 150+ fulfilling the Department of the Air Force, Air Force Materiel Command, Air Force Research Laboratory, 711th Human Performance Wing, USAF School of Aerospace Medicine&#39;s&nbsp;recurring requirement for Aeromedical Aviation Laboratory (AAL) program support at Wright-Patterson AFB (WPAFB), Ohio.</p>\n\n<h4>BACKGROUND</h4>\n\n<p>The United States Air Force School of Aerospace Medicine (USAFSAM) located at Wright-Patterson AFB (WPAFB), Ohio, trains 100% of the USAF&rsquo;s Flight Surgeons in an Air Force Specialty Code (AFSC) awarding course entitled Aerospace Medicine Primary (AMP). The AMP course is composed of three increments: AMP 101, AMP 201, and AMP 202. AMP increments 101 and 202 utilize an Aeromedical Aviation Laboratory (AAL) to introduce AMP students to military flight operations. The Aeromedical Aviation Laboratory curriculum is specifically designed to expose flight surgeon students to the rigors of flying, mission planning, modern cockpit symbology, disorientation, instrument procedures, aerobatics, low-level navigation, and formation flight in a setting that closely mirrors USAF Undergraduate Flight Training (UFT). AAL provides each AMP 101 student with one flight sortie of one hour duration and AMP 202 students with 6 sorties totaling 8 flight hours. The purpose of the AAL program is to ensure all flight surgeon candidates receive flight experiences and complete the syllabus at Appendix A-2.</p>\n\n<p>The USAFSAM also sponsors a resident in aerospace medicine (RAM) program designed to enhance, motivate, and prepare these RAMs to make sound aeromedical decisions regarding aircrew member&rsquo;s fitness to fly, to evaluate physiological threats in the flying environment, and to research, identify, and design performance enhancements for aerospace vehicles and personnel. The AAL Aviation Competency Program (ACP) was developed to help accomplish these objectives. The ACP is a two year program and is broken out into two modules A and B. Module A includes 27 hours of academic training and provides each RAM with 14 sorties totaling 19.5 flight hours. Resolution (DRI&amp;R) process began migrating from the current system of record, the Deficiency Reporting and Investigating System (DRIS) to the Joint Deficiency Reporting System (JDRS). The Air Force (AF) has now begun capturing its deficiencies into JDRS. JDRS is a joint service adaptation of the existing Naval Air Systems Command (NAVAIR), Naval Aviation Maintenance Discrepancy Reporting Program (NAMDRP) system. This transition to the joint system was directed by the Joint Aeronautical Logistics Commanders (JALC) to improve joint visibility into deficiencies and critical safety items. JDRS provides the AF with a modern web and workflow based deficiency reporting system. JDRS now serves as the AF System of Record for the AF Deficiency Reporting Process, in accordance with Technical Order 00-35D-54. <br />\r\n<br />\r\nDeficiency Reporting is required through section 41, Public Law and is governed at the multi-component level through DLAR 4155.24. AF Policy Document 63-5 and AF Instruction 63-1201 established the requirement for deficiency reporting to be used to support the USAF Quality Assurance (QA) program and to maintain Operational Safety, Suitability &amp; Effectiveness (OSS&amp;E) baselines for AF weapon systems. AF Instruction 21-118 establishes the requirement for deficiency reporting to be used to support the Reliability &amp; Maintainability (R&amp;M) improvement programs for systems, subsystems, and end items. <br />\r\n<br />\r\n<strong>REQUIREMENTS:</strong></p>\r\n<p>Program includes:</p>\r\n<ul>\r\n    <li>Providing services to support JDRS for the AF that include, but are not limited to:\r\n    <ul>\r\n        <li>First and Second Level Help Desk Support </li>\r\n        <li>AF Centralized JDRS Account Management Support (User enrollment/Account Set Up) </li>\r\n        <li>AF Deficiency Process Subject Matter Expert (SME) Support </li>\r\n        <li>Lead Technical Role for the AF in support of JDRS and provide Technical Expertise and Guidance to the AF Deficiency Reporting Functional Customer and the Navy </li>\r\n        <li>Provide analytical support and retrieval of AF deficiency data to the AF Functional Customer for deficiency metric reporting and deficiency trend analysis </li>\r\n        <li>Support AF Deficiency Reporting Functional Customer&rsquo;s Requirements Process </li>\r\n    </ul>\r\n    </li>\r\n    <li>Provide JDRS Training for all AF Users, such as Process, Role and Tool Training\r\n    <ul>\r\n        <li>Responsible for maintaining the currency and accuracy of the AF JDRS Training Documentation </li>\r\n    </ul>\r\n    </li>\r\n    <li>Provide JDRS Technical Expertise to the AF Functional Customer and Navy for the AF System Interfaces such as, GCSS-AF Data Services, FINS (F-22/Lockheed Martin), L-3 Communications/Reconnaissance SPO, AFMC Logistics Information Center, Product Quality Deficiency Reporting (PQDR) Interservice Interface (II) &ndash; solution based on the Oracle e-Business Suite (EBS), for the USAF and the United States Transportation Command (USTRANSCOM) as established by the Assistant Secretary of the Air Force, Financial Management and Comptroller (SAF/FM). DEAMS provides accurate and timely financial information using standardized business rules and processes and complies with existing laws, regulations and policies. DEAMS is a long-term solution toward sustaining audit readiness and correcting financial reporting weaknesses. The Program and Functional Management Offices reside at Wright-Patterson AFB in Dayton, Ohio. Objective: Continued Development and Sustainment of the DEAMS Enterprise Resource Planning (ERP) System to achieve a fully integrated, auditable financial management system.</p>\n\n<p>The DEAMS program goal is to deliver a single financial system for United States Transportation Command (USTRANSCOM) Transportation Working Capital Fund (TWCF), the United States Air Force (USAF) and the Defense Finance and Accounting Service (DFAS) and to provide the following capabilities:</p>\n\n<ul>\n\t<li>Standardized business processes, rules, and architecture to enable production of consolidated financial reports</li>\n\t<li>Total asset visibility and accurate valuation of assets</li>\n\t<li>An unqualified audit opinion on every consolidated financial statement</li>\n\t<li>Reliable, relevant, and timely financial information for all levels of leadership</li>\n\t<li>Compliance with all relevant laws, regulations, and policies</li>\n\t<li>The one-time entry of all financial transactions</li>\n\t<li>Reduced dependence upon paper products and artifacts</li>\n\t<li>The elimination of legacy, stove-piped systems, and databases</li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>In 2005, the USAF selected the Oracle 11i eBusiness Suite as the core Commercial-Off-the-Shelf (COTS) Solution to meet the DEAMS Program Goal. USAF subsequently selected and contracted with Accenture LLC as the System Integrator (SI) for the Spirals 1 &amp; 2 (SP 1/2), Technology Demonstration (Tech Demo) Phase of the DEAMS Program. In 2007, the DEAMS Functional Management Office (FMO) fielded Spiral 1 (SP 1) at Scott AFB, IL. Commands resident at Scott AFB used SP 1, commitment accounting, in successful closeouts beginning in FY2007. The Government completed Spiral 2 (SP 2), hosted on the Global Combat Support System - Air Force (GCSS-AF), and delivered the capability to the end-users at Scott AFB and Limestone, ME in February 2010.</p>\n\n<div>The DEAMS Program Strategy is to enable USTRANSCOM, USAF, their Component Commands, and DFAS to utilize best-commercial-practice financial processes through the implementation of Financial Systems Integration Office Core Financial Systems Requirements and a compliant COTS system. an analysis tool that provides visibility to data that provides answers. The Web reports have limited customization but provide a quick means to view the data and the ability to locate variances in the program being queried. these six components is the Spectrum System Development Architecture (SSDA).&nbsp; The SSDA is an established framework that provides for consistent delivery and implementation of software system development using the SAP (formerly Sybase) toolset.&nbsp; The SSDA is not an application, and it does not dictate the implementation of any business solution.&nbsp; It is an architecture that brings a level of rigor, consistency, and predictability to the development of quality applications in a client/server and/or web environment to all of Depot Maintenance System Integration (DMSI) Components.&nbsp; It allows for DMSI to build standard, structured, and scalable enterprise level applications.</p>\n\n<p>The SSDA provides a standard infrastructure design for the DMSI components and a host of common application services.&nbsp; This infrastructure includes software components, class libraries, objects, System Business Transactions (SBT) templates, System Select Transactions (SST), and System Utility templates (SUT) proprietary to NCI Information Systems, Inc.&nbsp; These elements are implemented into Sybase PowerBuilder, EAServer, and Oracle Procedural Language/Structured Query Language (PL/SQL) and known as the SSDA Framework.</p>\n\n<p>The SSDA provides the following common reusable services:&nbsp; Administration Module, Application Level/role based Security, Error Handling, Transaction Management, Common GUI Look &amp; Feel, Object/Component Management, Application Scenarios, Application Partitioning, Broadcast Messaging, Report Engine, AF Portal Ready and Audit Logging.&nbsp; These basic infrastructure services are mandatory for any type of application/system and are used by all of the DMSI components which directly equates to a cost savings to DMSI because the components benefit from these common standard services without having to expend the resources and time to develop and/or maintain them across their application and/or the DMSI enterprise.</p>\n\n<p>The SSDA supports two distinct environments, client/server and web, and both leverages the SAP&rsquo;s open standards tools (i.e. Enterprise Application Server and PowerBuilder) and technologies.&nbsp; The SSDA also uses many other industry standard technologies and tools such as Java Server Pages (JSPs), JSP Tags, JavaScript, Oracle stored procedures, PL/SQL, XML, JavaBeans, Cascading Style Sheets (CCS), and smlPortal. It promulgates FMS policy and serves as the command's Foreign Disclosure Office (FDO) authority. It also administers the command FMS budget and develops the command's FMS POM input. The largest organization within AFSAC, the International Division (IAR) conducts the daily operations of FMS case development and execution. AFSAC also orchestrates Air Force Materiel Command product and logistics center support of security assistance needs to 104 countries and seven NATO organizations. <br />\r\n<br />\r\nThe center serves as a 'portfolio manager' for over 2,500 total foreign military sales cases valued at over $102.6 billion. AFSAC provides logistics support for weapon systems dating from the 1940s, such as the C-47, to modern-day systems, including the C-130, F-16, F-15, C-17 and the MQ-9 Reaper Unmanned Aircraft System. It is an operational-level data environment, serving on-line transaction processing applications for managing day-to-day operations in the AF aircraft maintenance depots. As operational applications are technically refreshed, they are enhanced with new user capabilities for reporting and on-line analytical processing (OLAP). fulfilling the Department of the Air Force, Air Force Materiel Command (AFMC), Air Force Life Cycle Management Center, Digital&nbsp;Directorate, Airspace Mission Planning Division&nbsp;requirement for&nbsp;mission planning system engineering and integration.</p>\n\n<h4>BACKGROUND</h4>\n\n<p>The Air Force Life Cycle Management Center/Airspace Mission Planning Division (AFLCMC/HBM) is&nbsp;developing and sustaining a Mission Planning system of systems (various Mission Planning Environments&nbsp;(MPEs)). Each MPE operates as its own system. In order to develop and support an evolving product line of&nbsp;Mission Planning components, the Mission Planning program office (HBM) requires critical engineering&nbsp;integration capabilities and explorations into methods to improve architecture design, tools, utilities and security&nbsp;patches/infrastructure to include security monitoring, configuration, and vulnerability management capability.&nbsp;Air Combat Command (ACC) is the lead command for the requirements activities of Mission Planning systems&nbsp;used across various Air Force platforms however, Army and Navy, and other agencies including Foreign&nbsp;Military Sales (FMS) contribute to those requirements in support of their individual missions. AFLCMC/HBM&nbsp;is the lead for the development and delivery of all MP products. Mission Planning products have the potential to&nbsp;support all DoD fixed- and rotary-wing aircraft. The Mission Planning user community includes intelligence&nbsp;personnel, aircrew, flight level managers, and force level planners for combat, reconnaissance, airlift, and&nbsp;refueling operations.</p>\n\n<p>Mission Planning involves the creation of a flight plan based on mission needs, threats, targets, terrain, weather,&nbsp;aircraft performance capability, and configuration. It is an essential task that must be completed prior to any&nbsp;fixed- or rotary-wing aircraft sortie. The planner must have the ability to plan the delivery of weapons, cargo,&nbsp;passengers, and/or fuel, as well as calculate fuel requirements and assess the route based on known enemy&nbsp;threat location and type. Mission planners must be able to optimize and de-conflict flight routes with other&nbsp;aircraft; review, print, and brief the mission plan; and load pertinent flight information to on-board aircraft&nbsp;avionics.</p>\n\n<p>The Mission Planning program office (HBM) works collaboratively with the Army and Navy to leverage&nbsp;technical solutions and business practices for all DoD platforms. It provides automated Mission Planning tools&nbsp;and product support for fixed- and rotary-wing aircraft and guided munitions. HBM is replacing two closedarchitecture&nbsp;legacy Mission Planning systems and the PC-based Portable Flight Planning Software (PFPS) with&nbsp;a single multi-service open-architecture system more commonly referred to as the Joint Mission Planning&nbsp;System (JMPS). JMPS allows aircrews to process larger amounts of data required for today&rsquo;s platforms more&nbsp;efficiently to be able to create that mission in an acceptable amount of time. Mission Planning delivers&nbsp;significant benefits to command and control (C2) performance by enhancing information superiority for the&nbsp;Warfighters and by providing unique capabilities in support of precision engagement.</p>\n\n<p>The Mission Planning program office operates with a streamlined development process between the&nbsp;Government, Mission Planning Enterprise Contract (MPEC) developers, and the Systems Engineering and&nbsp;Integration Contractor (SEIC). There are currently five (5) MPEC II developers on contract as of February&nbsp;2015: BAE Systems Incorporated, DCS Corporation, Lockheed Martin Corporation, Northrop Grumman&nbsp;Corporation, and Tapestry Solutions Incorporated.</p>\n\n<p>Mission Planning is framed by the &ldquo;early look&rdquo; approach of all developed software and defined by processes&nbsp;posted on information-sharing platforms such as Mission Planning Central (MPC), a SharePoint site. Early&nbsp;involvement with developers, open meetings and working groups, and open technical reviews allow the&nbsp;Government, MPEC (or other developer) and the third party integrator (SEIC) to effectively find advanced,&nbsp;integrated and Enterprise-wide systems engineering and innovative solutions in the Government&rsquo;s best interest&nbsp;as it pertains to low-risk ways to deliver improved cost, schedule and&nbsp;performance&nbsp;of&nbsp;each Mission Planning&nbsp;Environment (MPE) across the Enterprise.&nbsp;</p>\n\n<p>Mission Planning development follows a well-defined software factory process which emphasizes a Systems&nbsp;Engineering, software assurance best practices, and integration approach that includes open technical meetings;&nbsp;sharing of code/design/interfaces; unlimited Government rights to products; consideration of advanced IT<br />\ntechnologies, technology insertion collaboration across the Enterprise; early incorporation of development and&nbsp;operation test forces; and submittal of all code and build environments to the SEIC contractor for early&nbsp;evaluation of both source and executable code, all in the context of Enterprise functions. This process has&nbsp;developed over time and does change to incorporate the best available methods, tools and techniques. The&nbsp;process is recorded in the Process Asset Library (PAL) on MPC as well as other Enterprise plans and&nbsp;documents. The contractor shall assist in executing the current software factory process and to recommend&nbsp;improvements to the process as needed. This will include creating a secure code review methodology&nbsp;(leveraging manual, static, and dynamic analysis) as well as acquiring and using code analysis tools.</p>\n\n<p>A key part of the Mission Planning streamlined development process is the Integrated Baseline Approach&nbsp;(IBA), which serves as the basis of what becomes the Mission Planning Environment or JMPS Integrated Build&nbsp;Environment (JIBE). The IBA is a methodology used to allow for the Mission Planning Enterprise to pick the&nbsp;correct version of a component, considering the technology, compatibility, interfaces, and capabilities to&nbsp;support the end user needs. The IBA will consider the testing tools, processes, availability, and data as part of&nbsp;the approach. The software that supports the baseline integration approach is vetted by SEIC for operational&nbsp;effectiveness and suitability with the identified versions of Framework (FW), other components, tools or&nbsp;utilities. The need for the products like the JMPS MPE or JIBE, derived from the IBA, extends beyond MPEC&nbsp;developers: the JIBE enables organic developers and other contracted developers to create the FW, Common&nbsp;Components (CC), and Unique Planning Components (UPCs) that make up the current JMPS architecture. The&nbsp;JIBE is a product offering a solution of compiled JMPS Tools/Utilities/Security Patches and developed software&nbsp;components to expedite the development of MPEs. Currently, the JIBE is tailored to support specific aircraft&nbsp;requirements and vertical integration of the UPC by the developer. The result of a vertically integrated JIBE&nbsp;plus the UPC is a platform-specific MPE, deemed suitable and effective for fielding. The contractor supports&nbsp;integration, verification, validation, and evaluation of all components that make up the MPEs, the remaining&nbsp;sustainment of legacy systems, and the remaining effort to transition from legacy systems to JMPS.</p>\n\n<h4>REQUIREMENTS</h4>\n\n<p>The program scope includes Joint Mission Planning System (JMPS) Architecture Design, Security Patches and&nbsp;Utilities required to field and sustain individual Mission Program Environments (MPEs). The program scope&nbsp;also includes products to enable multiple development contractors to develop and produce MPEs that meet the&nbsp;JMPS System of System Architecture. The Systems Engineering and Integration Contractor provides systems&nbsp;engineering products and integrated builds across the Mission Planning Enterprise required to procure and&nbsp;deliver integrated and interoperable MPEs to the US Warfighter(s) and Foreign Military Sales (FMS)&nbsp;customers.</p>\n\n<p>Early involvement with developers, open meetings and working groups, and open technical reviews allow the&nbsp;Government, Mission Planning Enterprise Contract (MPEC) developer or other developers and the third party&nbsp;integrator (SEIC) effectively find integrated systems engineering and innovative solutions in the Government&rsquo;s&nbsp;best interest as it pertains to low-risk ways to deliver improved cost, schedule and performance of each Mission&nbsp;Planning Environment (MPE) across the Enterprise. This translates into five main goals of the Mission&nbsp;Planning Enterprise as it pertains to SEIC:</p>\n\n<ol>\n\t<li>Minimize the overall cost for developing, implementing, and operating Mission Planning Systems acros the Department of Defense (DoD) by way of the System Engineering Integration Contract</li>\n\t<li>Increase the speed of readiness for fielding improvements to Mission Planning Systems to increase&nbsp;operational capabilities (thereby improving Mission Planning Enterprise technical efficiency)</li>\n\t<li>Significantly increase the Cybersecurity and Cyber Resiliency of fielded Mission Planning Systems</li>\n\t<li>Improve the quality of Mission Planning products to improve software usability, performance&nbsp;reliability, availability, maintainability, openness and reuse</li>\n\t<li>Maintain optimum effectiveness across the full Enterprise life cycle and measure this effectiveness</li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>There are three primary objectives that will allow the Mission Planning Enterprise to attain these goals and<br />\nprovide the warfighter with a fully vetted and integrated MP product:</p>\n\n<ol>\n\t<li>Adequate domain expertise to ensure successful life cycle support, software development,&nbsp;management, systems engineering and integration, evaluation and test across the Mission Planning&nbsp;Enterprise.</li>\n\t<li>Effective development of a secure and resilient architecture design, utilities, tools, and installation/test&nbsp;scripts in the production and delivery of an integrated baseline of configured software products or&nbsp;designated MPEs.</li>\n\t<li>Effective communication and tracking of software and processes, leveraging a Government-approved&nbsp;information sharing platform for appropriate tools, utilities and other data, CRIS is a read-only, multi-tier, relational data warehouse providing top-to-bottom level visibility across all echelons of the Air Force.</li>\n\t<li>The CRIS warehouse offers data from multiple legacy systems: Budget, Accounting, Supply, Fuels, Flying Hours, Civilian Pay and Personnel.</li>\n\t<li>The breadth and depth of data reduces the data collection effort allowing more time to be spent on decision support. Approximately 14,000 users utilize CRIS warehouse either by a business intelligence tool or web application, and execute over 8 Million queries a year with an average query response time of 5 seconds.</li>\n\t<li>The CRIS program&rsquo;s mission is to deliver reliable, accurate and timely data.</li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>Scope:</p>\n\n<ul>\n\t<li>To provide maintenance and sustainment support for CRIS to ensure continuous system availability to over 14,000 Air Force Users.</li>\n\t<li>Support includes help desk services, server administration, development and integration of legacy code, interface maintenance, system and security updates and other associated activities required to keep CRIS in operational status.</li>\n</ul>\n\n<p><strong>Requirements according to the June 2015 Sources Sought:</strong></p>\n\n<p>The Commanders&rsquo; Resource Integration System (CRIS) is the data warehouse decision support and analysis solution that meets these Air Force objectives.&nbsp; CRIS is a centralized data repository of U.S. Air Force financial budget and execution, supply, and personnel and payroll data, populated by standard legacy systems.&nbsp; It delivers timely, accurate, and easy to use data while providing superior support to military operations.&nbsp; Users can see daily business transactions anywhere in the world at any time, with some transactions updated multiple times a day (near real time).&nbsp; CRIS operates on a cross-functional relational data warehouse, focusing on Secretary of the Air Force (SAF), Headquarters Air Force (HAF), command-level and base-level Financial Management (FM), Directorate of Operations (DO), and Directorate of Logistics (LG) reporting functions.&nbsp; Since its inception in 2001, CRIS has grown from an initial limited user base to a current user base of more than 16,000 users performing approximately 1.1 million ad hoc queries each month, with more than 90% of these queries executed in less than 10 seconds.</p>\n\n<p>With CRIS, users can design and submit their own queries, allowing them to retrieve exactly the data that they need to see.&nbsp; Approximately 98% of queries submitted to CRIS are ad hoc queries, making CRIS unique among data reporting systems.&nbsp; This ad hoc capability gives users the ability to receive only the data that is relevant to their question, enabling them to make more informed decisions.&nbsp; The primary purpose of the DM CODB is to consolidate and provide data for the production management of the Depot repair and maintenance process for the Air Force Enterprise. It is an operational-level data environment, serving on-line transaction processing applications for managing day-to-day operations in the Air Force aircraft maintenance depots. As these applications were modernized, technically refreshed, or rehosted, their data was loaded into the DM CODB. This environment then became their common source of data, receives their data updates, and sends and receives their data interfaces. The DM CODB does not absorb any of the primary functional logic of the DMSI application systems, but performs their data retrieval and updates, their interfaces, and scripts needed to produce the interfaces. Through these operational applications, the DM CODB supports AFMC users worldwide, including inventory management specialists, maintenance specialists, equipment specialists, fund monitors, and quality assurance specialists. <br />\r\n<br />\r\nThe DM CODB provides corporate-level data to support AFMC tactical and strategic planning activities. As operational applications are technically refreshed, they are enhanced with new user capabilities for reporting and on-line analytical processing (OLAP). Drawing upon the DM CODB&rsquo;s consolidated data, these systems enable users to extract information, perform analyses, create reports, and respond to information requests from AFMC command-level staff, HQ Air Force staff, and Department of Defense (DoD) elements. The DM CODB also provides data for similar purposes to the Air Force Global Combat Support System Data Services (GCSS-AF DS), and to other data marts / warehouses and Enterprise Integration projects. (MWS).</p>\r\n<strong>BACKGROUND<br />\r\n</strong><br />\r\nAeronautical Systems Center, Mobility Systems Wing (MSW), Large Aircraft Infrared Countermeasures Systems Squadron (LAICSS) awarded two next generation missile warning system (NexGen MWS) System Development and Demonstration (SDD) contracts in FY04.<span style=\"mso-spacerun: yes\">&nbsp; </span>This 28 month development contracts complete in FY06.<span style=\"mso-spacerun: yes\">&nbsp; </span>The LAICSS intends to procure production ready NexGen MWS systems for operation with the AN/AAQ-24(V12) and (V13) system starting in FY07.<span style=\"mso-spacerun: yes\">&nbsp; </span>The intent of this announcement is to determine if there are interested, responsible and qualified sources which can meet program requirements, improved missile warning systems for installation on the C-17 and other aircraft platforms.<span style=\"mso-spacerun: yes\">&nbsp; </span>The NexGen MWS production program will also include spares procurement and an interim contractor support period.<span style=\"mso-spacerun: yes\">&nbsp; </span>The estimated period of performance for the production program is FY07-FY12.<span style=\"mso-spacerun: yes\">&nbsp; </span>During this period, an estimated 150 NexGen MWS processors and 650 sensors; and required support will be procured.<span style=\"mso-spacerun: yes\">&nbsp; requirement of Department of the Air Force, Air Force Materiel Command, Air Force Life Cycle Management Center&nbsp;for the Capability Assessment of the Non-Developmental Light Attack Platforms in support of the Office of Strategic Development Planning and Experimentation (SDPE).&nbsp;</p>\n\n<h4>REQUIREMENTS</h4>\n\n<p>NOTIONAL LIGHT ATTACK REQUIREMENTS</p>\n\n<ol>\n\t<li>Aircraft Attributes of Interest (AAI)\n\t<ul>\n\t\t<li>a. Light Attack.\n\t\t<ul>\n\t\t\t<li>The aircraft must be capable of:\n\t\t\t<ul>\n\t\t\t\t<li>i. Continuously computed impact point (CCIP) and/or continuously computed release point (CCRP) employment of MK81/82, BDU-33s, rocket pod munitions (70mm Hydra), laser guided and if capable, GPSaided munitions</li>\n\t\t\t\t<li>ii. CCIP employment of a .50 caliber minimum forward firing aerial gunnery capability</li>\n\t\t\t\t<li>iii. Employment of a minimum of two 500 lb class weapons while configured with a forward firing gun and electro-optical (EO)/infrared (IR) sensor.</li>\n\t\t\t</ul>\n\t\t\t</li>\n\t\t</ul>\n\t\t</li>\n\t\t<li>b. Armed Reconnaissance. To allow for targeting and reconnaissance from both crew positions the EO/IR sensor should be integrated with the aircraft avionics, displays and heads up display (HUD). The aircraft must be capable of:\n\t\t<ul>\n\t\t\t<li>i. EO/IR Full Motion Video (FMV) imaging</li>\n\t\t\t<li>ii. Laser designation using North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO) Standard 1.06 micron laser designator</li>\n\t\t\t<li>iii. Night vision goggle (NVG) compatible IR marking.</li>\n\t\t\t<li>iv. Laser spot tracking</li>\n\t\t\t<li>v. EO/IR image downlink to distribute FMV line-of-sight in flight to Remotely Operated Video Enhanced Receiver (ROVER) or One System Remote Video Terminal (OSRVT) receivers.</li>\n\t\t</ul>\n\t\t</li>\n\t\t<li>c. Mission Performance. The aircraft must be able to take off using a maximum runway length of 6,000 ft, clear a 50-foot obstacle, and fly a combat profile using the Reconnaissance 1 (REC1) profile in Annex 3 for sea-level standard day conditions.</li>\n\t\t<li>d. Aircraft Availability. Aircraft must support an operations tempo requiring 900 flight hours per platform, per year, for ten years. The aircraft availability rate must be at least ninety percent (90%) Mission Capable (MC) for completion of day and night missions under Visual Meteorological Conditions (VMC). Platforms must be capable of sustaining an eighty percent (80%) Fully Mission Capable (FMC) rate for the completion of missions under Instrument Meteorological Conditions (IMC).</li>\n\t\t<li>e. Tactical Communications. Aircraft must be equipped with two Joint, secure tactical UHF/VHF voice radios. The UHF radio must be capable of using HAVE QUICK II. If cryptological hardware is not installed for the experiment, then secure communications capability will be evaluated on paper. Beyond Line of Sight (BLOS) voice communications and a tactical datalink (Joint Tactical Information Distribution (JTIDS) or Situational Awareness Data Link (SADL)) will be required on production aircraft; if not equipped for the experiment, this capability will be evaluated on paper.</li>\n\t\t<li>f. Range and Endurance. Aircraft must be capable of 2.5 hours mission endurance with appropriate VFR fuel reserves, full guns, and two weapons stations loaded with munitions. External fuel tanks are permissible. Ferry range with external fuel must exceed 800nm.</li>\n\t\t<li>g. Aircraft must have onboard EO/IR sensors that provide the crew with ability to detect, identify, track, and target stationary or moving surface targets under day or night conditions in clear weather. Aircraft must be suitable for NVG or equivalent night vision use by aircrew. Aircraft should be able to employ battlefield illumination devices (parachute flares). If SUU-25 is not appropriate for flight this capability will be evaluated on paper.</li>\n\t\t<li>h. Survivability. Aircraft will be evaluated for IR and visual signature during the experiment. Aircraft systems must provide awareness of attack sufficient to employ countermeasures effective against IR-guided and RF proximity-fuzed weapons (chaff &amp; flares). Missile warning will not be evaluated in the experiment and must be evaluated on paper. Engine and cockpit floor must be capable of being protected by armor capable of defeating a 7.62x39 BP round (GRAU designation 7N23) at 100 meters range; armor need not be installed for the experiment. Aircraft must be capable of having a radar warning system installed but one need not be installed for the experiment.</li>\n\t\t<li>i. Supportability. Aircraft must have an average fuel flow of approximately 1500 lb/hr or less, clean.</li>\n\t</ul>\n\t</li>\n\t<li>System Attributes (SA)\n\t<ul>\n\t\t<li>a. Cockpit Configuration. The configuration of each cockpit must allow for full control of the aircraft, weapons employment, weapons jettison, and weapons override from either seat, permitting single pilot instrument flight rules and visual flight rules operations by either aircrew IAW Title 14 Code Federal Regulation (CFR) Part 91. The aircraft will have or be capable of having a control system in the rear seat that allows sensor, expendables release, laser and radio control without having hands on the flight controls. The aircraft must have dual tandem zero airspeed and zero altitude ejection capability, accommodating pilots 64 inches &ndash; 77 inches tall and sitting heights of 34 inches &ndash; 40 inches. All aircraft flight and fuel controls, and critical/essential circuit breakers must be accessible from front cockpit, with seat belts/shoulder harnesses fastened. All aircraft controls and instruments within each cockpit must be visible and accessible from the seat, with seat belts/shoulder harnesses fastened.</li>\n\t\t<li>b. Navigation. The equipment must permit GPS and VOR navigation and flying localizer, VOR/DME and Category I ILS approaches in accordance with Title 14 CFR Part 91. The equipment should permit flying non-directional beacon (NDB) and azimuth direction finding (ADF) approaches, integrated GPS approach profiles, and be equipped with a database which can be populated with approach descriptions, digital maps, and digital terrain elevation data. Common multifunction display (MFD) cockpit configuration for all aircraft to include Wide Area Augmentation System (WAAS) and Vertical Navigation-certified Global Positioning System (GPS) navigation capability to allow day/night, all-weather instrument flight, especially into airfields that do not possess navigational aids. The aircraft must also have the ability to be FAA ADS-B compliant.</li>\n\t\t<li>c. Communications. The aircraft must have Mode C/S transponder or Mode C/3 transponder and have a crew intercom and dual radios to communicate simultaneously with Air Traffic Control (ATC) facilities and operational agencies line of sight (LOS) IAW Title 14 CFR Part 91. Aircraft must have or be capable of having an IFF transponder covering Mode I, II, IV and V. A control head must be provided to allow the crew to transmit/receive simultaneously on four (4) radios. This SA overlaps with the Tactical communications AAI at 1e.</li>\n\t\t<li>d. Environmental. The aircraft must be equipped with an air conditioning and heating system capable of cooling/heating the aircraft interior to 55 &ndash; 85 degrees F when outside temperatures range from &ndash; 30 degrees F to 122 degrees F to include solar gain. The environmental control system must be able to cool avionics to below 131 degrees F within 30 minutes of avionics startup, and must be able to indefinitely maintain the temperature of the avionics at 131 degrees F or lower. The cooling system must have sufficient dust and sand filters to be capable of conducting desert environment operations.</li>\n\t\t<li>e. Defensive Measures. The aircraft must be capable of employing manuallyactivated and automatic IR and RF countermeasures (flares &amp; chaff).</li>\n\t\t<li>f. Materiel Reliability. A 95% probability of completing a mission profile free from a mission critical failure in flight as outlined in Annex 3, paragraph D2 is required.</li>\n\t\t<li>g. Propulsion. The aircraft must have a propulsion system that will operate on JP-8 or Jet-A fuel and accommodate all takeoff, flight and landing operations with standard fuel and weapons loads as described in Annex 3. The aircraft must be capable of being started with an internal battery. In addition the aircraft must be able to start using an external power source including another aircraft of the same model.</li>\n\t</ul>\n\t</li>\n\t<li>Additional Attributes\n\t<ul>\n\t\t<li>a. Light Attack. Stores Loading and Carriage. The aircraft must be capable of carrying the standard conventional loads (SCLs) listed in Annex 3 on NATO/US compatible hard points IAW MIL-STD-8591 Design Criteria Airborne Stores Suspension Equipment and Aircraft Store Interface.</li>\n\t\t<li>b. Maneuverability. The aircraft, in a clean configuration with sensor ball, must be capable of performing the following maneuvers: aileron roll, cloverleaf, lazy eight, loop, barrel roll, chandelle, Cuban eight, Immelmann, and split S.</li>\n\t\t<li>c. Operating Ceiling. The aircraft must be able to operate up to 25,000&rsquo; MSL (FL250).</li>\n\t\t<li>d. Pressurization and Oxygen. The aircraft must provide pressurization for sustained flight up to and including FL250. The aircraft must provide a selfgenerating oxygen system to support flight up to FL250.</li>\n\t\t<li>e. Unimproved Surface Capability. The aircraft must be capable of taxi, take off, and land with a configuration as described in the mission profile in Annex 3, paragraph D1.2. on unimproved surfaces rated at a California Bearing Ratio-5 (CBR-5). Aircraft must be able to turn with no ground support other than fuel.</li>\n\t\t<li>f. HUD. The aircraft must have a HUD in the front and a HUD repeater with symbology and camera picture in the rear cockpit. HUD data must include aircraft flight parameters, position, navigation, timing, and weapons employment information.</li>\n\t\t<li>g. Mission Reconstruction. The aircraft must be capable of allowing timesynchronized digital recording and replay of cockpit audio, HUD, and MFD data from training missions for at least two hours. All recorded data must be available for playback on a laptop computer-based tool during debriefing.</li>\n\t\t<li>h. Mission Planning Tools. Aircraft mission planning must use a computer-based mission-planning tool and be easily transferred to each aircraft by a removable storage medium.</li>\n\t\t<li>i. Night Operations. The aircraft must be capable and have the necessary lighting to permit night formation flying. NVG compatible position, anti-collision/strobe, landing, taxi and interior lights are required. Cockpit instrumentation must be NVG compatible. Variable intensity standard NVG compatible interior lighting is desired.</li>\n\t\t<li>j. Operations in Icing Conditions. Aircraft must be capable of transiting light icing conditions for 15 minutes.</li>\n\t\t<li>k. Certification. Aircraft must be certified or capable of gaining certification for day/night visual flight rules/instrument flight rules (VFR/IFR) operations. Aircraft must be certified or capable of gaining certification to meet acquisition requirements and allow for U.S. Military System (ABSS).&nbsp;<br />\r\n<p><strong>BACKGROUND:</strong>&nbsp;<br />\r\n<br />\r\nABSS (HO21) is an Air Force-wide standard financial management system that has been deployed to all active duty AF locations worldwide and to all tasked Air National Guard, Air Force Reserve Command, and European Geographically Separated Unit locations. ABSS has used object-oriented analysis and design methods, and Commercial Off-the-Shelf/Government Off-the-Shelf (COTS/GOTS) applications when possible in the system life cycle.&nbsp;&nbsp;<br />\r\n<br />\r\nABSS currently operates in distributed, two-tiered, client/server architecture. The Oracle 8i database is resident on Sun E250/450/3000 Unix servers located at all 80 active duty Air Force bases. One hundred and twenty-nine Air National Guard, Air Force Reserve, and geographically separated units remote into the active duty Air Force Bases for their database support. The ABSS application is usually installed on organizational file servers/shared drives but may also be installed on the client&rsquo;s workstation. The ABSS Program Office performs centralized Unix Systems and Oracle Database Administration for 73 of the 80 database servers. Seven Air Force Materiel Command bases are locally administered. ABSS incorporated Public Key Infrastructure (PKI) utilizing a DoD approved COTS application (DBSign). In January 2001, a PKI-enabled version of ABSS was implemented and supports the paperless initiative by substituting digital certificates in place of hand written signatures.<br />\r\n<br />\r\n<br <li>Staffing and operating a help desk </li>\r\n    <li>Developing training materials and performing classroom instruction </li>\r\n    <li>Supporting Air Force-wide (worldwide), multi-MAJCOM systems Unrestricted"}],"contractTypes":[{"id":22,"title":"Cooperative Agreement"}],"country":"USA","createdDate":"2019-10-02T14:03:32.073","description":"<p>Bioindustrial Manufacturing And Design Ecosystem is fulfilling the Department of the Air Force, Air Force Materiel Command, Air Force Research Laboratory, Materials and Manufacturing Directorate - on behalf of the Office of the Undersecretary of Defense (OUSD) for Research and Engineering (R&amp;E)&nbsp;requirement for a Synthetic Biology Manufacturing Innovation Institute.</p>\n\n<h4>BACKGROUND</h4>\n\n<p>The OUSD(R&amp;E) has established Biotechnology as a Defense-wide Modernization Priority. A potential benefit offered to DoD by biotechnology is the ability to secure the supply chain for a wide range of non-medical products via biomanufacturing from domestically sourced feedstocks and reagents, including synthesized DNA. Other possible benefits include reduction in lifecycle costs by utilizing sustainable and self-renewing processes, enhanced or novel product performance, biosecurity, and potential for distributed manufacturing including in austere environments. Defense applications that may be addressed using synthetic biology-enabled manufacturing include but are not limited to small molecules or precursors of small molecules for monomers/pre-polymer/resins/coatings, materials to be used in extreme environments, energetics, electro-magnetic nanomaterials, catalysts (e.g., for fuel cells), structural materials (bio-inorganic hybrid materials), sensors, and living organism products (e.g., for growable biomaterials, bioremediation, bioleaching, probiotics, and sensors). The combination of defense priorities addressable by synthetic biology manufacturing and the commercial potential of these innovations to industry will require the expansion of a relatively small number of experts into an ecosystem and workforce capable of innovating and supplying U.S. defense and commercial requirements.</p>\n\n<p>The Department of Defense (DoD) is therefore seeking input from industry, academia, nonprofits, and other stakeholders as it considers the establishment of a Manufacturing Innovation Institute (MII) dedicated to synthetic biology (SynBio) for non-biomedical applications. The SynBio MII will seek to enable a wide range of commercial and defense-relevant products and applications that can be customized to continuously evolving DoD and commercial needs. The information provided under this RFI will be used to determine the viability of using a public private partnership business model and to select and scope the technology focus areas. The Department is requesting responses that will assist in refining the technology focus areas currently under consideration (section 5 below), based upon evidence of support for national security requirements, economic benefit, technical opportunity, relevance to industry, sustainability, and workforce development opportunities.</p>\n\n<p>Today, the MII network contains 14 Institutes that each have a specific technology or market focus, led by a not-for profit organization that includes domestic-based members from industry and academia. The goal of the SynBio MII would be to create an impactful public-private partnership enabling the advanced development and scale-up of emergent biomanufacturing technologies and processes with the goal of successful transition of existing science and technology into defense and commercial manufacturing applications.</p>\n\n<p>The SynBio MII would be distinct from two other existing MIIs associated with biotechnology: the Department of Commerce (DOC) sponsored National Institute for Innovation in Manufacturing Biopharmaceuticals (NIIMBL) and the DoD&rsquo;s Advanced Regenerative Manufacturing Institute: BioFabUSA. NIIMBL&rsquo;s focus is on innovation leading to flexible and agile manufacturing processes for biologic therapies to treat debilitating illnesses. BioFabUSA is focused on establishing large-scale manufacturing of engineered tissues and regenerative medicines. Because both NIIMBL and BioFab USA are focused on products ultimately for human use, the subject matter experts, regulatory environment, methodologies, and toolsets used to produce molecules and materials envisioned in the SynBio MII are substantially different than those employed by them. None-the-less, we anticipate the SynBio MII to interact closely and collaborate with all MIIs, including NIIMBL and BioFabUSA, to seek areas of common interest and reduce or eliminate areas of potential overlap.</p>\n\n<p>The overall goal of the SynBio MII is to reduce the cost and time to achieve robust biomanufacturing with a focus on fostering and sustaining a globally-competitive U.S. manufacturing base. It is anticipated that the SynBio MII will deliver:</p>\n\n<ul>\n\t<li>Commercializable amounts of target molecules, materials, or cell-based products through scale-up manufacturing and down-stream processing, and</li>\n\t<li>Platforms for testing and evaluation in order to rapidly and thoroughly assess and identify novel functionalities of target molecules, materials or cell-based products</li>\n</ul>\n\n<h4>REQUIREMENTS</h4>\n\n<p>The ManTech program invites responders to provide information in support of a potential future solicitation for a SynBio MII. While current MIIs are led by non-profit organizations, MIIs are ultimately industry-driven in order to be sustainable over time and promptly address both commercial and defense needs. Therefore, all responsible sources are encouraged to submit information that shall be considered by the Government.</p>\n\n<p>We ask respondents to align their inputs with one or more of the three following technology focus areas of interest: A. Scale-Up Manufacturing (SUM), B. Down-Stream Processing (DSP), and C. Testing and Evaluation (T&amp;E). Response should address the general questions listed in section 5.D. below as they relate to each technology focus area of interest addressed</p>\n\n<p><strong>A. Focus Area 1: Scale-Up Manufacturing (SUM)</strong> For the SUM topic, the DoD is interested in approaches that facilitate domestic biosynthetic production of molecules (organic molecules, biopolymers, inorganics) and cells. Central to synthetic biotechnologies is the engineering of chassis organisms that are designed to reliably perform an intended function. However, while cellular behavior/yield/growth may be optimized in small-scale systems, engineered organisms often do not behave predictably when introduced into larger scale fermentation systems or other scale-up methods (e.g., via distributed systems or co-location with domestic feedstocks). To increase the use of engineered organisms in manufacturing, the community needs better tools for scale-up manufacturing, including models to relate lab-scale and full-scale fermentation, analytics, and reactors that monitor and adjust growth conditions, metabolites, yield, and other parameters in real time.</p>\n\n<p><strong>B. Focus Area 2: Down-Stream Processing (DSP) </strong>DSP refers to post-fermentation characterization, processing and purification of biosynthetic products or cells. In order to advance bio-manufacturing, separation techniques (e.g., tangential flow filtration, distillation, etc.) need to be scalable and commercially-viable. DSP can be performed after small, mediumor large-scale fermentation, and also is essential to confirm that a desired product has been synthesized or cultivated, that it represents a certain fraction of the total products, and that desired purity has been achieved. Novel separation technologies, perhaps borrowed from the chemical engineering and/or materials science communities, may be required. Purification of unique bio-made products (e.g., intact cells) may require development of more tailored, purification tools that are designed for these types of products.</p>\n\n<p><strong>C. Focus Area 3: Testing and Evaluation (T&amp;E) </strong>The ability to rapidly assess and characterize emergent molecules, materials, and cells across a battery of tests and evaluation procedures will be critical, and will also facilitate identification of novel defense applications for biotech-enabled products. The required evaluation platforms present major challenges, in part because of the sheer diversity of new materials, molecules, and cells that will emerge from design-build-test biotechnology platforms. Comprehensive T&amp;E platforms that harness state-ofthe-art analytical tools for characterization, assessments, and measurements of bio-produced molecules, materials, and cells will underpin successful biomanufacturing of these types of products.</p>\n","duration":"FY20 - FY27 (Start 'FY' may vary)","govEntity":{"id":24607,"title":"MATERIALS AND MANUFACTURING
